The suspense is part legal thriller and part police procedural. We follow Bosch and Haller each an expert in their own field as they process the many ups and downs and twists and turns of a very controversial and demanding case.<br /><br />The author continues to push the boundaries of crime fiction by redefining and joining two exciting protagonists with different backgrounds into a legal quagmire. The story is told with chapters that go back and forth in time and alternate from first to third person, they condense decades of time into a compelling narrative that explores various elements of L.A.'s criminal justice system.<br /><br />The story reopens a twenty four year old case in which little Melissa Landy was abducted from the front yard of her Hancock Park home while playing hide and seek with her sister. At the time, tow truck driver Jason Jessup was convicted of her murder but modern day technology, DNA evidence has led to the reversal of Jessup's conviction. But not everyone is convinced...<br /><br />Haller, a wisecracking cynic and highly competent grizzled veteran of countless courtroom battles switches from defence to prosecution and as readers we shadow him through countless courtroom shenanigans. 0380977273|3.0|When a late carnival comes to town on the heels of a storm, two boys find themselves pitted against the ominous Mr. Dark in an ancient battle against the night. Bradbury is one of my favorite authors, but I don't have much fondness for his nostalgia literature--like Something Wicked This Way Comes, or Dandelion Wine. My memories aren't made up of those landscapes or experiences, and so there's no personal fondness. I also begrudge that branch of his work its stylistic redundancy. Bradbury's science fiction has incredible atmosphere, but it's driven by a demanding premise and/or constrained to short form, and so there's less opportunity for the prose to run away with itself--instead, his emotional focus and quietude are atmospheric blessings. Something Wicked has fantastic elements, but they're powered by nostalgia--and so while it offers up some beautiful turns of phrase, those are lost within a mountain road of prose. I've read the book before, some years back, and find I remember some parts well: there's aspects of the book that I love, and Bradbury creates a veritable carnival of horrors which delight me; I see how they could make for a successful book. Bradbury portrays an intensely idealized but vibrant representation of youth and youth's confrontation with age (and with life)--and his themes, despite their lack of personal appeal, are carried through with conviction. But no matter my best intentions and my love of the author, Something Wicked isn't the book for me. I appreciate and can recommend it for what it tries to be, but I don't enjoy it.

B005LAIGFA|2.0|There is both good news and bad news in this movie. First the good news: Leonardo diCaprio is a certified master craftsman. If he had not already proven so in \\"Aviator,\\" (which I think he did), now there is simply no remaining doubt that he is the brightest star in our contemporary Hollywood firmament. Whenever an actor can take a crappy script, such as this one was, and turn it into a mini-master piece, that actor has arrived at the gates of superstardom. That is exactly what Mr. diCaprio has done in this movie.<br /><br />He has also done one additional thing, and this is the beginning of the bad news. He has served, wittingly or not, as a slave to the ugly sadistic intents of a director whose goal was always to minimize and to whitewash Hoover's sordid past; and thereby diCaprio is also complicit in trying to humanize America's only certifiable homegrown monster.  I do not believe that they succeeded at this goal. And as a result, in that failed attempt, both diCaprio and the director, Clint Eastwood, have a lot to answer for.<br /><br />Although we have seen this trope before -- of pawning off cheap faux patriotic heroism on an unsuspecting public -- this director knows all too well that when the historical facts of Hoover's past are lined up and are coupled with even a smidgen of Freudian analysis, our nation's ugly past suddenly becomes an open book. It becomes all but transparent that America's closet (occupied by J. Edgar) is opened for inspection. And at center stage of the darkest chapters in our nation's criminal history, sits none other than the prickly, little evil, mean-spirited, cross-dressing, closeted mommie's boy, J. Edgar Hoover.<br /><br />The fact that we allowed him to remain in the closet so long and to bully us all the while he was there, is itself profound testament to our collective national cowardice. That a movie director would hedge in the direction of making this monster a respectable condensation symbol and a backhanded national hero,is just short of unconscionable. Even Communist Russian came clean about Stalin's crimes, and summarily executed Hoover's equivalent, Laventia Beria.  Eastwood has made Hoover into a wimpy mother's boy G-Man hero, but a hero nonetheless. And he is not even deserving of that. Portraying Hoover as a national hero - even as a limp-wristed whiney one -- is like turning our pedophile uncle into a family hero? It is no longer possible for us as a nation to maintain our collective virginity in the face of 40 years of allowing J. Edgar Hoover to intimidate us and allowing him to trample on our democratic freedoms in the false name of crime-fighting. Because we lacked the courage to challenge his tyranny, we simply wittingly became part of Mr. Hoover's closet.<br /><br />Here in a contorted \\"half outing\\" of Hoover, a minimally honest and confusing script is presented for our evaluation. It is everywhere self-evident that the director of this movie obviously lacked the courage to take the only stand that could be taken with respect to J. Edgar Hoover: Eastwood had to take a full step forward and tell the whole, the complete, unalloyed truth about the embarrassing deeds of the monster, Mr. Hoover evolved into. Allowing it to seep out along the pathway that led only to the sewer did not exactly do the job. That only betrayed the fact that even in death we still fear him. This man had 40 plus years to hold our nation in utter terror.  He is no hero; he is a bona fide villain.<br /><br />This was Eastwood's grand opportunity to finally help us purge the nation of Hoover's ghost. But Eastwood did not do this. Instead, he tried in vain to cover all of Hoover's many failings as FBI Director -- as well as his many personal indiscretions. Mr. Eastwood's rendition of him in this film is little more than an exercise in \\"soft-pedaling\\" around all the landmines left by Hoover's mean-spirited and embarrassing indiscretions. For Eastwood, the only one worthy of display was his obsessive hounding of Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. -- that is, until Dr. King was eventually assassinated -- many believe at the hands of Hoover's own FBI and under Hoover's direction. But sadly, even with DiCaprio's great acting, this did not make the movie add up to anything but a very forgettable squander of a grand opportunity to get our national history correct.<br /><br />Here are some of the other disturbing facts that did not (but should have) come out in the movie about Mr. Hoover:<br /><br />Because the mob had clandestinely photographed him in full flagrante fellatio delecto mode at a party where he was also on display as a female in a \\"Little Bo Pete\\" outfit, this in itself is strong evidence that as a \\"cross-dressing\\" closeted homosexual, Mr. Hoover was probably under the control of the mob during most of his career?  The only hint of this in the movie was the single instance in which RFK, Hoover's boss, called him in to ask why he was not investigating the mob instead of racking up statistics on auto thefts? That, plus a single conversation between Hoover and his live-in boyfriend, Clyde Tolson, about his annual trip to the Del Mar racetrack, near San Diego, Cal.<br /><br />Why make this a muted, nuanced secret reserved only for us insiders? Is it so well known that Hoover vacationed annually, free of charge (and was even staked in the races) with mob money that it could be subtly insinuated instead of having been spelled out in bold letters?  It seemed to me that the truth demanded that the public at large know that our nation's chief law enforcement officer was vacationing at the behest of the very mob that he was supposed to be investigating? How could the casual viewer not be told that Hoover was doing this at the same time that he was claiming that the mob did not exist?<br /><br />Of course no mention was made in the movie of the fact that Hoover was one of the attendees at the pre-JFK assassination party/planning session on 11/21/63 at Clint Murchison's Dallashome. The FBI director was there along with a whole host of others high-level movers and shakers, including Nixon, LBJ, the CIA and other governmental and mob officials -- all eventually implicated in various JFK's assassination plots.<br /><br />Nor was there any mention made in the movie of how much Hoover (like the mob) hated his boss RFK. Of all his many slimy and underhanded Machiavellian capers, the only one that got any attention, in addition to his relentless hounding of MLK, was his subtle blackmailing of Presidents Roosevelt, Kennedy and Nixon in a successful effort to get his tenure extended. In the JFK and LBJ case, it was to get it extended beyond the automatic retirement age of 70.<br /><br />Also, how could the movie not mention that for twenty-five years Hoover and LBJ were virtually next door neighbors, and partners in crime; or that, at the very least, they were both implicated in the cover-up (if not the murder of JFK)?<br /><br />But even more important is that during the Cold War, at the height of the spy wars, Hoover and the FBI as our chief counter espionage agency, caught no spies: that's right zero, nil, zilch, as in none. Even the Nazi spies shown in the movie as being captured during WW-II had actually turned themselves in. Hoover's FBI also caught no Mafioso either, that is, until RFK turned up the heat on him.<br /><br />The upshot of this movie is that by failing to purge the Hoover monster from our collective consciousness, he remains a part of us; a metaphor about the collective monster within. Not having purged Hoover's ghost of course leaves open the possibility that another Hoover could arise from his ashes.<br /><br />This was a disturbing movie, but for all the wrong reasons. I no longer trust Eastwood's courage as a director. Ten stars for DiCaprio's acting, and two stars for Eastwood's directing.

1455510319|5.0|Michael Connelly is, first and foremost, an experienced crime journalist and an expert on police and courtroom procedure. \\"The Fifth Witness\\" focuses on Micky Haller, Connelly's hard-bitten defense attorney, and how his business has evolved in the economic downturn since 2008. With less money around, Haller has shifted to handling foreclosure cases - helping home-owners to delay or frustrate attempts to evict them for failure to pay what they owe. It's akin to a shark feeding on small fry: each case brings in only a small fee, and Haller's small team has to do a Red Queen act - running as fast as they can just to stay in business. So, naturally, it's like a gift from the gods when one of Haller's foreclosure clients is accused of murdering a VP at the bank that owns her mortgage and is seeking to take her house. The publicity alone is manna from heaven, and Haller hopes to arrange some kind of media deal - maybe even a Hollywood contract. Then things start to turn sour, as his neurotic client starts making her own moves and queering his pitch. Add to that the brilliant, ruthless DA who is leading the prosecution (and has already beaten Haller in a number of cases), and his always-fraught private life, and Haller is soon up against it. But things rapidly get worse - much, much worse - in more ways than one.<br /><br />Like all Connelly's novels (and particularly those starring Haller) \\"The Fifth Witness\\" is more gripping than exciting. It gradually shapes up as an intense personal duel between Haller and Andrea Freeman, the prosecuting attorney, in which they both use every possible trick and technique, whether fair and ethical or not. It is here that it seems to me Connelly's fundamental bias shows just a little. Having worked with the police in his career as a journalist, and written so much about them as a novelist, he is inclined to take their side; and that makes Haller a fundamentally less self-assured, confident person than Connelly's detective hero Harry Bosch. Whenever a prosecutor or a cop makes a crack about how Haller defends criminals and scumbags, it sinks right home and he worries about the ethics of his business. When talking to his new associate Jennifer Aronson, he stresses that a defense attorney should never ask whether the client is guilty or innocent: it's better not to know. And that, of course, means that when he wins a case he cannot take satisfaction in having saved an innocent person from prison, because he does not know whether the client really was innocent. The prosecutors, for their part, take exactly the opposite attitude: if the police hand them a suspect, they assume that person's guilt right from the outset. So it becomes clear that the courtroom battle is not really about guilt or innocence, or even about justice as most of us would understand it. Rather, it is a battle or a game played within certain rules, where winning is all that matters. These dark reflections on the state of criminal justice (in California and, no doubt, elsewhere) permeate \\"The Fifth Witness\\" and give it a thought-provoking ethical dimension that most legal thrillers lack. (John Grisham's books, especially \\"The Runaway Jury\\", are obvious exceptions).r a prosecutor or a cop makes a crack about how Haller defends criminals and scumbags, it sinks right home and he worries about the ethics of his business. 1451648537|1.0|I suppose it's healthy to learn that one's all-time favorite hero has warts and I expected a fair and complete portrayal of Steve Jobs--the good, the bad and the ugly.  But as \\"the only authorized biography\\" of Steve Jobs wore on, it became clear that the author was intent on skipping over the good in favor of focusing almost exclusively on the bad and the ugly. I don't deny that I am among Jobs' biggest fans, and assume that most of us who admired him from afar have long been well aware of his idiosyncrasies. But I believe it was unnecessary to have his failings repeated and reinforced, page after page, ad nauseam. At the beginning of the book, I actually thought Isaacson both liked and respected Jobs. By the end, I became convinced that Isaacson disliked Jobs personally and actually harbored contempt for him as a husband, father, brother, son, entrepreneur, manager and human being in general. Why else would he have included so little insight from those who knew him the longest and loved him the most?  If Jobs was such a monster, why did at least 3 people say he was their very best friend (Jony Ive, Larry Ellison & Mona Simpson)? John Lasseter says Steve was like a brother to him. And countless others, including Al Gore, Bob Iger, Art Levinson and Tim Cook, remained close friends with Steve for decades. Either they are all wrong about Jobs, or Isaacson simply failed to include their unique perspectives in favor of all the old, worn-out stories about what a meanie Steve could be.  I haven't read his biographies of Ben Franklin or Albert Einstein, but unlike these previous subjects, Isaacson was given the exclusive opportunity to interview a live subject and tell us what it actually felt like to be Steve Jobs. Apparently this was not Isaacson's goal. Rather, I believe he fancies himself as a historian who was merely charged with the telling of history. And the result would be no different had Jobs already been gone for 100 years or more--just like Isaacson's previousworks. I don't dispute anything that's contained in this book, but I do question why so many positive personal insights were completely left out in favor of the slow and steady drumbeat that Steve was a jerk--first, last and always. I believe Isaacson's main goal in writing this dry, incomplete, one-sided monstrosity was to persuade readers to dislike Jobs as much as he did. It is biased beyond belief, and therefore, cannot be considered a serious historical accounting.  The book felt disjointed in the way it was broken into chapters according to the development and launch of Jobs' companies and products, such as iMac, Pixar, iPhone, etc., rather than in true chronological order. This caused a great deal of going back and forward in time to the point where I had to stop reading and actually Google certain events, just to keep the timeline straight in my mind. The actual date of one key event in Jobs' life, the acquisition of Pixar by Disney, was missing altogether. Telling the story of Steve Jobs' life according to his creations (which often overlapped), rather than by a more cohesive chronology, is reminiscent of the way mercenary hackers dissect each new iThing the moment it goes on sale, so they can disseminate which supplier's components are inside. Such data may be valuable to Apple's competitors and Wall Street, but says nothing of the essence of the product--or in the case of this biography, the essence of Steve Jobs.  In addition to the lack of a cohesive timeline, there were instances of a lack of context. For example, on one occasion, Steve Jobs gets a call from then-President Bill Clinton, asking Jobs what he should do about the Monica Lewinsky scandal. Jobs tells him that if it's true, he needs to tell the truth to America. No doubt, this is an interesting tidbit, but there was no reference in the book that the two men had ever met one another, let alone built a close relationship that one would expect from this type of interaction. Without any type ofcontext provided for their relationship, either before or after this phone call, its inclusion in the book seems more like name-dropping than anything else. And if Steve Jobs and Bill Clinton were such good friends and confidantes, then wouldn't the next obvious question be to ask Jobs how he balanced his friendships with Clinton and Ross Perot (Jobs' backer in NeXT), when they were running against one another for POTUS--the highest office in the land? Sorry, folks. No mention of any such thing. Just the phone call that seemed to come from out of nowhere. Another question left unanswered is how Al Gore came to serve on Apple's board of directors. There is no mention whatsoever of the two men having any kind of relationship at all.   Of all his amazing accomplishments, Jobs believed his greatest legacy was his family and the company he built. But you'd never know it by reading this biography. The love of his life, Laurene, barely got more mention than his old flames, Chrisann Brennan and Tina Redse. And both of these old flames received far more attention than any of his three children with Laurene. Jobs' entire relationship with Patty, the sister he was raised with and who was also adopted by Clara & Paul Jobs, was relegated to one sentence saying the two had never been close. But if this is an accurate representation of their relationship, why did Patty hold vigil at Steve's bedside in the final days and hours of his passing? Likewise, there was no mention whatsoever of the passing of Steve's beloved dad, Paul.  Jobs told Isaacson that he wanted to live to see his son Reed graduate, and the book briefly mentions that after Reed's graduation, the family had a graduation party at which everyone took turns dancing with Reed. Yet, a neighbor mentioned briefly in a blog post the night Jobs retired from Apple that he was so proud of Reed, he had tears streaming down his face during the graduation ceremony. Mona Simpson said in her brother's eulogy, \\"None of us who attended Reed's graduation party will ever forget the scene of Reed and Steve slow dancing.\\" The distinction to me is that Isaacson's description was almost a non-event. But two other people--in just a single sentence from each--provided enormous insights into the same event and how much Reed meant to Steve. This simple distinction best exemplifies Isaacson's historical textbook style of writing, in which he seems incapable or unwilling to include many of the warm fuzzies that were also true of Steve Jobs.  The final 20 pages of the book appeared to be thrown together in Simon & Schuster's rush to capitalize on Steve Jobs' death after witnessing the worldwide outpouring of love and grief. Originally scheduled for March 2012, the publishers moved up the publication date to October 24--just 19 days after Steve's passing. And it shows.  Almost anyone could have written an historical account of Steve Jobs' amazing achievements. But Jobs gave Isaacson the rare opportunity to share much more than this. Just as Steve Jobs designed the iPad 30 years before it was launched, and always knew what the world would want well before it did, Steve also knew that the world didn't need another book about him that merely dwelt on his creations and character deficiencies. He gave Isaacson two precious gifts that he had never given to any other human being: 1) authorization for the biography and unprecedented access with over 40 interviews and countless emails and phone conversations; and 2) complete trust in the telling of his life's story--a supremely difficult and courageous thing to do for someone who guarded his privacy and knew no other way than to micromanage everything with which he was associated. Wouldn't his life's story be much more precious to him than anything he'd ever accomplished? With these facts in mind, Isaacson clearly whiffed on this once-in-a-lifetime opportunity in favor of being petty, biased and small.  There's no question in my mind what Steve would have thought ofthis book. While it may be true that the key to Steve's genius was all about \\"what he left out,\\" sadly, the same cannot be said of Walter Isaacson, because the precious pieces he left out of Steve's life's story say more about the author than the subject. In the end, it may well be that Walter Isaacson is just the latest on the long list of people who betrayed Steve Jobs. However, it is worth the read for its interesting tidbits, but only if you put the book down occasionally to bring it to life by watching online videos of Steve's iconic product launches, TV commercials and other interviews in which Steve quite clearly spoke for himself. At least for now, if you want to know Steve Jobs, watch his Stanford commencement speech and read Mona Simpson's eulogy for her brother.

1451648537|5.0|Perhaps one of the best things to say about this biography is that my immediate inclination is to write about Jobs rather than about the book about Jobs.  Isaacson's lens in recording Jobs' life is unobtrusive; the focus remains on the story rather than the telling.  This skilled technique also wins the readers' trust: because the reader feels that she is seeing Steve Jobs directly, she trusts the impression that she is getting.  Thus, if Isaacson has presented a slanted or misleading picture in some way. . . well it would be very effectively done.   If Isaacson has misled us (which I doubt) though, it is not by making Steve Jobs likeable.  To the contrary he comes off as a jerk and less of genius than he seemed like going in.  The book places Jobs in the pantheon of Edison and Ford rather than Franklin and Einstein.  No mean company to be sure, but down here with the mortals.   More than anything else, this is a book about leadership. Jobs' leadership turned around a failing company and led it to the status of most valuable company in the world.  The up-close view of his techniques in doing so--his absolute insistence on excellence (\\"A players\\" to work with; great products as the goal), his insistence on endless editing (refining products again and again and again until he could not see any way that they could be better), his insistence that not only every detail but every aspect (e.g., the details of the box the product comes in; the color of the machines in the factory) of the product from end to end is critical, his insistence that the world was as he would have it be rather than as it is (his so-called \\"reality distortion field\\"), and his secure insistence that great products are things people did not yet know that they wanted--is fascinating and highly instructive.  It does leave you with an understanding about what was so extraordinary about this guy.   I found the personal side of the book less interesting.  We learn a great deal about Jobs' life, and for atrained psychologist, perhaps enough to figure out what made him tick.  Not enough for me to do so though.  The picture here is of a guy deeply lacking in empathy and with a bizarre set of compulsions, but, notwithstanding the well-filled out personal story, I finished uncertain where they came from.  Not that I cared terribly much.  Jobs' life was a mildly interesting identity story and personal journey-adopted child, college dropout, life in India etc.-but cast next to his professional life, it necessarily pales into ordinariness.   I should add that the book was an easy, fun and accessible read.  Actually hard to put down, which is pretty good for biography.  I come away from it looking differently at both my Apple products and the world around me (that pizza box is crap!).  No need to be a technology person to enjoy this book.

1400052181|4.0|When I first read the back of the book, I thought the topic was very interesting, but I worried that an entire book might be a little too much.  BUT, I was pleasantly surprised. The book focuses more on the human story of HeLa, than the scientific importance.  The science is definitely there, but it is explained in a way everyone can understand and relate too.<br /><br />Henrietta was a poor black woman who grew up working on a tobacco farm with her extended family.  Shortly after the birth of her fifth child, she found out she had cervical cancer.  This was in 1951, she was treated, but the cancer spread and she died.  Samples of both her healthy cells and cancer cells were taken by scientists at John Hopkins when she was treated, and the cancer cells were the first human cells to survive and keep reproducing outside the body.  This was revolutionary, and the cell line called HeLa changed the world in many different ways.  Everytime Rebecca Skloot lists off scientific advances that have used HeLa, it is impressive.<br /><br />Even though they were responsible for taking the cells without asking or informing Henrietta and her family, I really enjoyed reading about George Gey and his wife Margaret.  I liked that Gey built his labs out of scrap metal and  invented amazing things for scientific research without patenting them.  He sent the HeLa cells to anyone who was using them for scientific research.  I feel like his motives were good, he wasn't looking for fame, he was just a really smart guy that was obsessed with science.  I enjoyed the simplified explanations of how cells were used, and how specifically HeLa's cells allowed scientists to study so many different things.<br /><br />Henrietta's family really suffered after she died.  Her children were mistreated and abused by the cousin who came into take care of them. Three of her kids were still really young when she died, and an other one had been institutionalized for epilepsy and other illnesses.  Luckily, the oldest brother married a woman, Bobette, that figured out what was going on and rescued the kids.  Sadly, the damage had already been done.  Add to that poverty, un-diagnosed hearing problems and struggles with the law, and her children had really hard lives.  When they were asked for blood samples years later, bits and pieces of the history of their mother's cells were explained to them.  But not explained well.  They each struggled with it in different ways.  I think their reactions are well-justified, and as they slowly confide in the author and explain their experiences, you can really understand why they each reacted they way they did.  Some of the descendants wanted financial retribution, which is also understandable since they are all struggling with different illnesses, and many don't have health insurance.  Ultimately, they just want people to know who their mother was, and better understand themselves.<br /><br />Much of the story comes from Deborah, who was only 2 or 3 when her mom died.  She has a lot of spunk, and definetly grows on you as she searches for information about her mom.  I think the biggest tragedy in the book is not that the cells were taken, but that those 5 kids lost their mom when they were so young.  I'm sure they would have still had struggles, but if they had had Henrietta to love them and teach them, I think things would have worked out better.<br /><br />Rebecca Skloot explains different advances in patient privacy and disclosure.  She outlines different agencies' attempts to prevent what happened to the Lacks family from happening again.  But doctors can still store and use your cells without you knowing.  As I read the different issues and concerns, I wondered how I would feel if I found out 20 years later that my cells had been used.<br /><br />I recommend this book.  It will be different than anything else you've read.  It is very interesting, educational and thought-provoking.a woman, Bobette, that figured out what was going on and rescued the kids. Ultimately, they just want people to know who their mother was, and better understand themselves.     Much of the story comes from Deborah, who was only 2 or 3 when her mom died.  She has a lot of spunk, and definetly grows on you as she searches for information about her mom. But doctors can still store and use your cells without you knowing. 1451648537|1.0|As a technologist and Investor interested in the company of Apple, I am less interested in the peculiarities of Steve Jobs personality, and more interested in the way he thought and how he invented and made decisions.  In this respect the book is lacking and left me wishing and waiting for a better book that provides more of THAT type of information. The author spent a tremendous amount of time with Jobs and seemingly lost an amazing opportunity (granted to no one else) to help business people and technologists understand how one of the greatest inventors of our time invented. Hence the rating of 1 Star... if that is what your looking for, go elsewhere. PLEASE will someone write THAT book! Consequently I do not recommend this book to anyone... in fact I tell my friends not to waste time reading it.  If you do read it, then know why you are reading it. At times it almost feels like a hit piece and one wonders what Jobs did to tick off the Author? The impression it left me with was of Job's life as a series of hissy fits punctuated by some useful business insight. Unfortunately it is very light on insight and focusses extensively on the emotional side of things, with little time given to Jobs mental and intellectual approach to invention and product. I might distill the insight down to a few salient points: a) Be a perfectionist, b) Hold a high standard and push people to achieve it. c) People DO judge a Product by its appearance!  How do you take vast chunks of time between products, or with companies ... in many cases years... and boil it down to a few vignettes illustrating where and how he ticked someone off in that phase of product development... who cares!! I 'm more interested in the fact the product exists, why it exists, how it came into existence, what WAS done vs. what WAS NOT done, and why.  There is simply not enough of that in this book and I found myself as I read constantly bemoaning the lost opportunity.  There ARE few interesting vignettes about histime with Ives and their creative process. That subject alone will likely make a tremendously useful and interesting book some day. But unfortunately THIS book always comes back to a vignette of some lesser persons emotional reaction to Jobs or a product at one of those creative times. Hey... they rioted in the streets of Paris when Stravinsky released Rite of Spring! Do we care?!  Typical example of what I increasingly found annoying:  I lost count of the times the book made mention of Jobs \\"Reality distortion field\\" as if this was a flaw in his character?! Wut?! Is that not the sign of a great leader or inventor?! Most people are trapped in their mediocrity and conventionality and limited capacity for vision and then a Steve Jobs comes along and says we CAN do THIS. Lesser people say NO it's not possible, but he goes ahead and does it anyway by pushing people beyond whatever limitation they suffer from (something often requiring a size 12 boot properly applied) and drives them to do it. So I ask: who had the reality distortion field?! I would suggest it was not Jobs. Also I might point out something the Author does not seem to understand and only mentioned in passing - Jobs studied and spent a LOT of time in and around Zen Monasteries and with a Zen master. If you know anything about Zen then you know one of the meditation practices is that the Roshi will whack you with a stick whilst meditating and attempting to reach enlightenment. The reason being two-fold a) primarily trying to get you to forget your petty little ego and its pissing and moaning about this and that, and b) trying to get you to forget your body. Both for the same reason: so that you can focus on the real task at hand and gain enlightenment. NOTE: it is well understand in such circles that the petty little ego is what obstructs realization. And it is somewhat akin to the ego busting that takes place in the Army. In both cases the end result is universally recognized as better... but for some reason not in the work world. I wonder what percentage of the ass whup'n jobs handed out was a Zen Boot camp thing.... I bet more of it than some might like to think. Anyway, the author seems to have entirely missed that angle, although Jobs did hint at it numerous times. I often wondered why the Author never explored that angle? That also would be interesting to know and he could have asked anything?  Anyway, Jobs was a great inventor, who had the ability to be so in touch with the state of the technology and the materials and medium and environment in which he worked that he saw possibilities where others didn't. Yet mostly this book is harping on emotional drama and side notes (often repetitively) and it really did start to annoy.  In closing I guess what I will say about this book is: if you are looking for a lot of business insight into a great technology inventor and company go elsewhere, if you want to read about some of the drama in the life of a great technology inventor and his company, read this.

1451648537|4.0|What Steve Jobs showed with the iPod, the iPhone, and then the iPad was a preternatural sense of not only what consumers what they wanted but what they needed and the unique design sense to build an end-to-end integrated vehicle to deliver exactly what they needed. What author Walter Isaacson shows with his detailed treatment of his eponymous subject is a laser-sharp focus on the events and beliefs that constituted a tempestuous life singularly committed to what he saw. Where this voluminous biography falls short is a depth of reflection and distance on Jobs' legacy that feels less like an oral history and more like considered judgment on a life whose destiny feels otherwise fulfilled. The book is essential reading for anyone fascinated by Steve Jobs' success, but it also reflects how difficult it is to uncover genuine insight from a deeply enigmatic visionary.<br /><br />The one inarguable insight Isaacson provides is that Jobs didn't invent many things outright. He was a master at putting together ideas, art, and technology in ways deliberate in his mind, invented the future. His significant breakthrough was designing the Mac after appreciating the power of graphical interfaces in a way that an established computing giant like Xerox was unable to do. He later replicated this mindset with the iPod after recognizing how having access to a thousand songs in your pocket could bring joy in a way that Sony could never even aspire much less accomplish. Isaacson points out that most company leaders can push innovations by being dexterous at the big picture and delegating others to master details. Jobs did both with unfettered dedication and knew like very few could the most fruitful intersection between technology and art. Producing technology requires intuition and creativity, while making art requires rigorous scientific discipline. Isaacson tells us Jobs' innate gift was to marry science and art and then market what he created with unparalleled acumen.<br /><br />The facts of Jobs' short life are clearly presented here. He grew up in Palo Alto with an adopted father he genuinely loved and shared his love for all things mechanical and electronic. Becoming your typical high school geek, he saw college was not for him and eventually worked for Atari as a technician. While at Atari, he took seven months off, traveled to India, and experimented with LSD. Jobs came back to Atari and through a computer-geek club, met his eventual partner Steve Wozniak, who built the first Apple computer. Together, they started Apple Computer, and Jobs makes $100 million before he turns 25. Due primarily to a power struggle with CEO John Sculley and a penchant to launch expensive forays into untested products, he lost control of Apple, a life-changing event that provided the impetus for his subsequent success. In 1985, he bought Pixar from George Lucas in a bargain deal and with director John Lasseter, turns it into a wildly successful animation brand. In an amazing comeback, Jobs returned to Apple in 1996 and saw the advent of the web as the conduit along which to build a vertically integrated consumer electronics company with computers at the hub and a succession of revolutionary new devices as the spokes.<br /><br />What is considered here is impressive to say the least. Having died with a personal fortune of more than $8 billion, Jobs achieved what only Bill Gates has done as one of only two pioneers of the PC age, having created and built arguably the world's most famous and valuable company, Apple. In the process, he managed to touch all our lives. A visionary as ruthless and driven as any of the great first-generation American capitalists, Jobs has led a life that comes across almost like a modern-day fable with a multitude of strange and coincidental details. He infamously drove his collaborators to distraction with his perfectionism and at the same time, he empowered them to do their best. You are left to believe that Jobs had a level of self-awareness that far outweighed the scarifying temperament. Isaacson's portrait is revealing considering that Jobs waived his right to read the book before he died, but there is no getting around the inevitable deification that this level of intimacy evokes.e scarifying temperament. Isaacson's portrait is revealing considering that Jobs waived his right to read the book before he died, but there is no getting around the inevitable deification that this level of intimacy evokes.

B004EPYZQC|4.0|Fast Five adds an interesting twist to the Fast & Furious franchise, leaning more towards heist flick than a street racing film.  Universal Studios has breathed new life into the series by switching up the formula, and Fast Five won't disappoint action junkies.<br /><br />Fast Five reunites Dom Toretto (Vin Diesel) with cop-turned-racer Brian O'Connor (Paul Walker) in the favelas of Rio de Janeiro.  Diesel reprises his role of Toretto, this time on the run from a high-octane federal law enforcement team led by Luke Hobbs, played by Dwayne Johnson.<br /><br />Complicating their situation further is Brazilian drug kingpin Hernan Reyes, portrayed by veteran actor Joaquim de Almeida of \\"Desperado\\" fame.  The only way to dig themselves out of their hole is to pull off a final caper to the tune of $100 million, aided by a variety of former series characters.<br /><br />What makes the latest addition to the Fast & Furious franchise a success is the amount of adrenaline injected into the film.  Fast Five is chock full of tense moments, fast cars and shootouts.  Adding a bit of levity to an otherwise hardcore action flick are the duo of reggaeton artists Don Omar and Tego Calderon, who return to the series with comedic relief in tow.  Tyrese Gibson, Ludacris, Jordana Brewster, Sung Kang, and Matt Schulze also reprise their roles to offer a sense of familiarity to viewers.<br /><br />Fast Five's musical score is a mix of high-energy tracks with greater variety than other soundtracks from the franchise.  The film's score doesn't feature as much American rap as earlier films, though it makes up for it with the inclusion of urban Portuguese-language songs from Brazil.  International music ranges from Rio's rap to Brazilian Funk, plus the infectious \\"Danza Kuduro\\" by Puerto Rican reggaetonero Dom Omar, who returns to the film as the pessimistic Rico.  Fast Five is the third film of the franchise that composer Brian Tyler contributes to, with songs ranging from cinematic to industrial rock.<br /><br />There are two elements of the film that stand out.  The first being the too-good-to-be-true clash between Dom and federal strike team leader Luke Hobbs.  The two reaching their inevitable on-screen collision hits with the force of a scud missile soaked in testosterone.<br /><br />The other element is the final car chase of the film.  Gone are the intricate CGI shots of the mechanical workings of the cars involved. But what Fast Five loses in computer imaging, it makes up for with hardcore driving.  The ultimate chase scene in the movie is far from what you've seen in other heist flicks, and is sure to please even the pickiest of action junkies.<br /><br />That's not to say that Fast Five is Oscar-quality, of course.  To truly enjoy this film, you have accept in advance that this isn't Shawshank Redemption.  While much of the cast have experience in film and television, the majority serve up averageacting at best.  I'm a huge fan of Vin Diesel's movies (Pitch Black FTW), but he looks all of his 44 years in this film, and his acting ability hasn't improved much.  While he and Paul Walker both are well-suited for action flicks, don't expect profound performances from either of them.  Diesel is given more of an opportunity to flex his acting chops in Fast Five, with it's emphasis on relationships and heartbreak, but I honestly wasn't too moved.<br /><br />Dwayne \\"The Rock\\" Johnson carries the greatest screen presence of the entire cast, giving off an air of intensity that resonates throughout every scene.  But that being said, he wasn't given an opportunity to show off any real range of emotions in the film.<br /><br />Despite Fast Five having it's fair share of action and suspense, the film does drag on a bit longer than necessary with it's runtime of two-plus hours.  While the film's conclusion is fairly clear, it's also given an open-ending that paves the way for the franchise's sixth effort, and the possible return of a significant character from the series.<br /><br />Overall, Fast Five is simply a fun watch, a guilty pleasure if you're a fan of heist flicks or action movies in general.  If you're a motor head, you might be a bit let down at the abandonment of the franchise's original formula, though the change still make for a welcome spin on the series.  Definitely give this one a rent, or get the Blu-Ray/DVD combo to own one of the better movies in the series.fan of heist flicks or action movies in general. B0054NQZ9S|5.0|The sobering tale about a group of company people at a big manufacturing firm who lose their jobs during the Great Recession. The focus is mostly on Ben Affleck's character who thinks he can find another job but soon finds himself struggling to secure a job at half his salary and failing that he finds he has to work for his brother-in-law fixing houses. Over time he loses his house and car and many other things but especially his dignity. In the meantime there are some subplots as an older higher up exec finds himself in trouble because he's pushing 60 and one of the owners finds himself torn between his broken duty to his ex employees and the fact that his stock has shot up. It's a tale about how a lot of us were let down by the Big Whigs and we're the ones mostly suffering for it. It's also about how we as a culture put a lot of our identity into our jobs and toys and find ourselves sinking into depression when we lose those things. It's also about the humiliating process for some who have to take whatever job they can find but on a brighter note it's about the American Spirit and how we spring back up when we're supported by our loved ones. The cast is overly impressive: Tommy Lee Jones, Chris Cooper, Ben Affleck, Kevin Costner, Rosemarie DeWitt, Craig T. Nelson, Maria Bello and some other great supporting actors. It cost $15M to make but has yielded only a bit under $5M at the box office. Why did it yield so little if it's a great film? It had a limited exposure at theaters to begin with and it's a topic Americans don't want to encounter at the movies. That said, it's mirror of our times for many and it's also well done. Directed by John Wells in his feature film debut. I suspect a lot of the actors and actresses were moved by the script and offered to do it for far less. That says something about the picture even if it didn't yield bling bling at the box office. Lyrics aren't very good, and this is also another forgettable song.  Where The Wild Things Are 8/10 easily my favorite song on the album.. It's a mix of heavier and softer, mostly mid tempo. The chorus isn't as obvious, but flows well with the song. It's pretty long, probably still longer than it should be, but overall I really like this one, which I didn't so much at first, so it's a nice surprise to make the tops on the list, for this album.  Prince Charming 6/10 there's absolutely no reason this song should have reached 6 minutes, 4 1/2 at most. This might be the fastest song on the album. It's another heavier one. I like the vocals during the chorus, where it gets a lot of its attention from me. Musically it's ok, as is vocally, except for being too long. The lyrics drag this one down, or I'd give it a 7, possibly an 8. But for an 8 it'd have had to be shorter. It's fairly basic, and needed to be shorter. Still a decent listen.  Low Man's Lyric 4/10 I really want to like this one, but I don't. It's easily the softest song on here. It's also the most experimental. They use some different instruments, heard it once recently but already forgot it. Overall though, it doesn't do anything, thus it's boring. Also I'm not sure James's voice was right for this type of music. Overall there were some nice ideas, but it just didn't pan out very well.  Attitude 4/10 should've covered the Misfits's song of the same name instead. This is another rock song, but again, it's not very good. Some vocal parts as well as lyrics, rip off Fuel, which is a much better song.  Fixxer 4/10 is another extremely long song, given the simplicity of it. It's ok I guesss, but again, way too long. The chorus doesn't really do much for me either, and it's a very luke warm way to end an already blah album.  Overall this album is what it is, a b side collection from the Load sessions. Fans will find their hidden gems here, and those may vary. Fans of only the 80's stuff will hate this one. I definitely don't like the direction they took, but my tastes vary, despite what some would say, enough for me to find something to appreciate on here. Overall I'd say this album should only be for fans of the Load album who just want more of that. It's not for anyone else. They ripped people off by passing it off as a new album, when it's a b side collection, and the songs show. There are definitely songs that should've made Load. I wish they'd combined them into 1 album, taking Fuel, The memory remains, Unforgiven II, Where the wild things are, and maybe Prince charming and putthose songs on Load, and replaced that stupid country song amongst other bad songs on that album.  So I'm amazed at how bad this album is, but not unexpected given what it is. If you loved Load and want more of that, love everything Metallica does, or just have to have every album Metallica puts out, then feel free to get this. Just don't expect anything more than Load leftovers, and a couple songs that should've been on there.

B0009ELZAG|5.0|Brian Wilson once said of this album he wanted to write songs on \\"more than one level\\". And since the term \\"classic album\\" is often overdone and often used without care,Brian's own terms seem to define this album very much. And there are very few full lengh albums over the years that I can say exist on more than one level. The works of Marvin Gaye,Stevie Wonder and The Beatles come to mind. This is one of those records I can put on with absolutely no urge to skip over any songs. The key to the entire success of this album was arrangement. Inspired by the vocal harmonies of pop and doo-wop groups along with the wall of sound production of Phil Spector Wilson created this,which basically amounts to his debut solo album co-staring The Beach Boys on vocals and harmonizes. Aside from that Carl,Dennis,Mike and Al had precious little to do with the music on this album. That's where the name comes from. These were,in fact Brian's \\"pet sounds\\". Based on musical influences that impacted on him personally.<br /><br />  This album is home to some truly wonderful songs. Much heard and well known hits such as \\"Would'nt It Be Nice\\" and \\"God Only Knows\\" being among them. And interestingly enough if those songs excite and amaze you,than the rest of this album will be right up your alley. Much as The Beatles [[ASIN:B0025KVLTM Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band]] was supposed to be (but which even John Lennon admitted it actually wasn't) this album is completely musically and thematically unified. This is not the work of an adolescent celebrating California surf culture. This is the work of a reflective and yearning 20 something who'd by this point already experienced physical abuse,mental illness and was now married with a baby on the way. Speaking for a generation obsessed with the eternal youth,this sets the record straight on that. On \\"I Know There's An Answer\\",\\"Caroline No\\",\\"Here Today\\" and beautifully chorded pieces such as \\"I Just Wasn't Made For These Times\\",\\"Don't Talk\\" and \\"That's Not Me\\" there's this prevailing sense that the singer-songwriter who once mused about the delights of being \\"in my room\\" now saw the music world as forcing him to pay a big price for non conformity.<br /><br />  Musically? Well on this you here an album that goes for redefining what rock and roll was. Rather than having a lot of distorted guitars in the mix,playing three noisy chords you have elaborate organ/piano/horn/rhythm based songs that are more about melody,harmonic and chordal invention that making noise and \\"rocking out\\". On the instrumental title track for example you realize this album has a lot more in common with types of Northern soul recordings from Dusty Springfield or Georgie Fame for example. It's not by any means a guitar rock album. Bands such as the High Llama's would later make music very much based on this sound. But it definitely extends off the Beach Boys surf sound into something that comes very directly from Brian Wilson's soul. He would in fact suffer something of an emotional breakdown whilst attempting to outdo himself with the follow up album to this. That also mixed with the fact the blood and sweat perfectionism Brian put into this product wasn't fully appreciated by every member of the Beach Boys. 0553805371|4.0|As a non-scientist, one of the joys of reading Stephen Hawking, indeed the principal joy, is his unique ablity to write on complicated scientific subjects in laymen's terms which anyone of intelligence can understand. In this respect he reminds me of Bertrand Russell. Like Russell he elevates my intellect and makes me feel like I am much more intelligent than I really am.  In \\"The Grand Design\\" he does it again and I feel greatly edified by his treatment of both \\"determinism,\\" and the age of our universe. I feel like I have learned something new or, at least something I did not fully appreciate before.  He believes that human behavior is indeed determined by the laws of nature - i.e. determinism - but that the outcome is determined in such a complicated way and with so many variables, as to make it impossible in practice to predict. Since we cannot solve the questions that determine our behavior,  we use what is called an \\"effective theory\\" that people have free will. The study of our will and the behavior that arises from it, is the science of psychology. Economics is also an effective theory, based on the notion of free will plus the assumption that people evaluate their possible alternative course of action and choose the best. That effective theory is only moderately succcessful in predicting behavior because, as we all know, decisions are often not rational, or are based on a defective analysis of the consequences of the choice. That is why the world is in such a mess (and why there are Republicans.)  On the question of the age of the universe, it is obvious that for us to exist the universe must contain elements such as carbon which are produced by cooking lighter elements inside stars. The carbon must then be scattered through space in a supernova explosion, and eventually condense as part of a planet in a new-generation solar system. In 1961 physicist Robert Dicke argued that this process takes about 10 billion years. According to current data, the big bangoccurred 13.7 billion years ago.  In this book the authors quite clearly give us the latest cosmological thinking on the beginning and age of our universe, the Big Bang, General Relativity, Quantum Mechanics, and the ultimate theory of everything (if indeed one exists.) Their \\"candidate\\" for the latter is M-theory. I am not a physicist but I do not find their arguments for M-theory compelling. First of all they admit that M-theory \\"is not a theory in the usual sense but a whole family of different theories, each of which is a good description of observations only in some range a physical situations\\"; \\"No one seems to know what the M stands for\\"; and they conclude their book by stating: \\"if M-theory is confirmed, we will have found the grand design.\\" To me this seems too speculative and suggests that the only thing missing from their title was a question mark.

0262731541|1.0|This book has unusual provenance.  According to its author it originated in the work of a colleague, Frank R. Whitt.  Wilson claims that Whitt abandoned the manuscript and charged him to see it through to publication.  The first edition was credited to Whitt and Wilson, so we must accept Wilson's word for this.<br /><br />This edition, the third, was, again according to Wilson, originally to have been produced with another author, Jim Papadopoulos, who allegedly withdrew by reason of other interests.  He has been recognized, however, for \\"contributions.\\"  (See the review above by Papadopoulos for further information.)<br /><br />I find these representations about Whitt and Papadopoulos troubling.  I have read an authoritative technical article on the subject of steering, handling, stability of bicycles by Papadopoulos; his work is the best part of the third edition of Bicycling Science.<br /><br />More troubling is appellation of the word \\"science:\\" Wilson is an engineer; I am a physicist; I see little that is scientific.  It is a review or a tutorial, recapitulation of the work of others, with extensive bibliographic references.  Such is ethically permissible, so long as the originators of the content are acknowledged, and Wilson cannot be faulted on that score.<br /><br />But there is a plethora of inadequacies and outright mistakes too.  For example, Wilson deals with frames as if they were triangulated trusses with pinned joints, when in fact they are exactly the opposite, rigid frames, with maximal forces at the joints.  Butted tubing and lugs recognize this by placing most material at the joints; greater cross-sections there compensate for larger forces and so keep stresses within elastic limits.  This is why carbon-fiber frames also have bulky joints; design and size of joints is indicative of the properties of frame materials.  Almost everything in this chapter is misleading or wrong, typical of nearly the entire book.<br /><br />I suspect that most of whatis right in \\"Bicycling Science\\" is the work of Whitt and Papadopoulos.  In short, \\"Bicycling Science,\\" with David Gordon Wilson as author, is tendentious, sophistic, superficial, spurious, ephemeral.  It contains precious little engineering and no useful science.<br /><br />Do not buy it.  Buy \\"Bicycle Design\\" by Mike Burrows instead; it contains ten times the useful information in one-third the pages without errors.  Burrows has designed and built ingenious bicycles that actually work, many of which have been proven competitively, technically, commercially, so he knows what is right and what is wrong, what works and what does not.<br /><br />Also the title is ungrammatical: \\"Science\\" does not engage in \\"Bicycling.\\"  Sloppy usage.  Typical and indicative.

B0052REUW0|4.0|\\"Violence has declined over long stretches of time, and today we may be living in the most peaceable era in our species' existence.\\" (Location 128)<br /><br />This is, perhaps, the primary sentiment of this latest work by Prof. Steven Pinker.  And for all my reservations with his ideology (progressivism) and his lack of critique surrounding statistical analyses I cannot help but feel this is a brilliant, insightful, and profoundly optimistic book.  Optimism is something which has been, intellectually, in short supply for a great many decades. Though this seems odd because between the Long Peace (since the end of the second world war) and the New Peace (the end of the cold war) this is something we should all be feeling about our collective future.  However, pessimism seems to be the general sentiment: from Climate Change to the geopolitics of China and India.<br /><br />Back to The Better Angels of Our Nature.  The book is not without its faults<br /><br />-An out of control Progressivist ideology<br />-Feminization theories which are deeply suspicious<br />-Left Wing political agenda...as read by Americans<br />-A mostly uncritical attitude to statistical analyses (some is suggested but mostly as a sop)<br />-The inherent `squishiness' of the Social Sciences<br />-An aggressive atheism which discounts all the positives attributes of Religion in favour of Old Testament blood thirsty hyperbole (and some from the New Testament). Personal note: I'm an atheist.<br />-A rather hostile discounting of conservatives and conservatism (again, American in perspective): see page 662 and following for a sample of this.  Note: I read the Kindle Edition on my iPad.<br /><br />For all of the above weaknesses and others, this is still a timely, important, concise, and articulate piece of optimism...with provisos (Pinker's not mine).<br /><br />Perhaps some of Prof. Pinker's most interesting observations are why he believes the world remains pessimistic about violence.  One of my favourites is why the world, generally, feels violence is getting worse when it is actually declining:<br /><br />\\"The decline of violent behaviour has been paralleled by a decline in the attitudes that tolerate or glorify violence, and often the attitudes are in the lead.\\"  p. 155<br /><br />Our tolerance for violence appears to have fallen faster than violence actually has.  This, perhaps, could force a greater decline in violence or it could have unforeseen negative consequences.  At the moment this remains uncertain...to my way of thinking.<br /><br />This is a book which deserves several book length reviews and much closer analysis than I have given it here but it is brilliant and articulate while occasionally slipping into arrogance and ideological myopia (see progressivism and atheism amongst others).<br /><br />I did not give this book a full 5 stars because of the problems I have cited above, but it is a brilliant and important work that is both accessible and comprehensive (if the reader is willing to put in a little time).<br /><br />Highly Recommended<br /><br />Addendum<br /><br />There are two other reasons I only gave this book 4 stars and that has to do with the book's formatting.  1) References/End-notes are not activated 2)The index is not activated either.  These are two very serious problems for readers of the e-Editions of this book.  Some may feel this is not the place for this criticism but I would, respectfully, disagree because it makes looking up sources, so that I may engage more fully with the text, laborious, time consuming and uncertain. This is the typical way publishers handle eBooks...haphazardly at best. 0486650677|5.0|Lanczos' \\"Variational Principles of Mechanics\\" is an erudite piece of work that basically reconstructs the science of analytical mechanics bottom up, from the principle of virtual work to Einstein's equivalence principle and the origin of the gravitational redshift of spectral lines. The book contains very little material on the Newtonian, vector mechanics, being entirely devoted to the Lagrangian and Hamiltonian approaches to mechanics.<br /><br />The book provides a perfect introduction to the foundations of the all important principle of least action that pervades all of modern physics. In this regard, a nice companion to Lanczos' book is the treatise by W. Yourgrau and S. Mandelstam, \\"[[ASIN:0486458881 Variational Principles in Dynamics and Quantum Theory]].\\"<br /><br />One major \\"functionality\\" of Lanczos' book is to bridge the gap between our modern way of thinking and that of the classics. Anyone who has already tried to read the 1762 papers by Lagrange on Miscellanea Taurinensia or the 1834-1835 papers by Hamilton on the Phil. Trans. Royal Soc. knows that the classical literature is difficult to follow, part because of old-fashioned notation, part because we lack (well, at least I lack) the spirit of the times, making it difficult to understand some seemingly byzantine questions that the authors pose and ponder on in some of their (sometimes rather lenghty) writings. The book by Lanczos helps a lot both in terms of notation and ideas.<br /><br />I totally disagree with those that try to compare Lanczos' and Feynman's styles. Lanczos is seriously concerned with the history of the ideas, their evolution, and interpretations. There is nothing like that in Feynman's \\"The strange theory of light and matter,\\" or in his \\"The character of physical law,\\" and very little of it in his lectures on physics. Actually, Feynman pays very little credit to the historical development of the subjects. I am not saying that he personally does not recognize the credits, only that he does not communicate it. He admittedly has a \\"do it yourself\\" (or \\"did it myself\\") attitude towards physics, while Lanczos has a visible admiration for the greatness of his subject (without being cheesy) and is sensitive to its philosophical nuances and implications. I feel the difference between the two is like the difference between getting trained and getting educated.<br /><br />In summary: a must have in anyone's scientific library (unless you are an undergrad student cramming for your finals).he does not communicate it. He admittedly has a \\"do it yourself\\" (or \\"did it myself\\") attitude towards physics, while Lanczos has a visible admiration for the greatness of his subject (without being cheesy) and is sensitive to its philosophical nuances and implications. B005LB1MY6|4.0|Being that their last album was filled with absolutely amazing songs and some filler, I was happily surprised that this album sounded more like a true album. Although there isn't as many stand out songs, there also isn't as many fillers. Overall there is a theme of dance music mixed with big 70's big arena guitars which I love, although sometimes it doesn't work. Sometimes the songs need to be bigger and more thematic and sometimes they need to be more dance oriented, but over it's a good merry-meant of genres. There's something to be said about 70's arena rock and Dance put together. 70's arena rock was meant to be big, loud and cocky much like some dance that is big, loud and cocky, and that's why it works so well together. Standout songs for me are Civilization, Canon, On'n'On, Parade, News Lands, Helix and the albums title track.   But...I do have to say there are some problems with this album. First off the version of Civilization on the album is by far the lesser version of the song. Look for the 4:11 version which sounds better, better mix and is longer. In the 4:11 version when song is about to cut out it comes back for one more big chorus which I love. The other big problem with this album is that it's a product of the loudness war. Most dance or electronica albums usually don't suffer from clipping and over compression and usually leave the sound intact, but some of the bigger bands of this genre tend to try to make their albums compete with popular music. If you're not familiar with the loudness war, it just means that record companies try to make the cd sound as loud as possible thinking that listeners will respond better to that. The problem with that there is a a limit of how loud an album can be on cd before it clips off the music and distorts. This will cause the album to be distorted, lose clarity, and actually make the songs sounds very small and thin at louder levels. Believe me when I say that it may be one of the reasons people will like the album less then they should. Being that this music is supposed to be big large 70s arena rock dance music it greatly suffers from sounding too compressed.  Overall there is some amazing songs on here. The sounds of retro rock with contemporary music is absolutely amazing at times and you would be doing yourselves a disfavor by not buying this album.

1594203059|4.0|I bought this book on the spur of a moment, expecting some rather bland cultural comparison. How wrong I was. this is a remarkably readable book. Fergusons basic premise is that the West did win the 'War of Civilizations' and that this overall was a good thing. He marshals numerous examples as proof for western victory: Western clothing has become the universal clothing pattern, christianity is truly widely represented also in the indigenous population of all continents, western models of doing business, of organizing the military etc. etc. are being used all over the world.   The reason why the West did win he boils down to 7 'killer apps': competition, science, property rights, medicine, the consumer society, work ethics. He convincingly argues that these 'apps', as he calls them, were key to western superiority. He is not the first one to argue along these lines, for most of them others have argued along similar lines but most have done so in only one dimension, explaining western success only with one 'killer app'. Furthermore he boldly claims that overall western superiority and progress was a good thing. Yet he does not stop there. Basically he then goes on to ask: Now that others are successfully copying the western model, where will that lead us? On this front he sees a rudderless West blindly tumbling towards decline and oblivion, without showing a convincing scenario of 'what after'.  Overall I agree to his message. Western success has been much maligned by cultural relativists and yet the near universal acceptance of western culture points to something more than mere imperialsm. Yet there are a few caveats, which stopped me from giving him five stars. First of all, western domination is, according to him, Anglo-Saxon and more specificaly British. This means that he actually does not refer to the 'West' but to Britain. This he does with a jarring jingoistic undercurrent. Just to quote an example. Fergusan claims that on in 5 African soldiers in the Frencharmy in WWI perished, while French soldiers only had a mortality rate of 17%. Well, 1 in 5 is 20%, which does not seem to be awfully different from 17% and does not indicate racism to me - I can accpet that less experienced soldiers from tropical countries might suffer a slightly higher mortatlity rate in the cold fields of Flanders. He then goes on to point out that the mortality rate among British Indian troops was half that of British soldiers. Does he seriously try and suggest that the British were behaving more nicely towards their colonial subjects than towards native Britons?   He also dwells (rightly) on the horrors of colonialization, yet he prefers to specifically dwell on the horrors of French and German colonialization, which strikes one as ironic given the fact that Britain ran the largest empire of them all.  Thus one is left with a challenging, interesting polemic, which unfortunately relies to much a British perspective.
B005EHNEDO|5.0|Wilco is a band that has yet to not fulfill their fan's expectations. They are a band that, despite kicking around for a considerable time (in excess of 15 years I believe), they keep on beautifully balancing artistry, experimentalism and accessibility.<br /><br />As a devoted fan of the band, I have no problem with unequivically stressing that this is Jeff Tweedy's band. This is challenging music yet in a different manner from masterpieces \\"A Ghost in You\\" and \\"YHF\\".<br /><br />After repeated listenings, the high quality started to sink in and I started to understand what makes this great.<br /><br />I feel that what sets \\"The Whole Love\\" apart from the other works is that great aspects of the band (particularly Nels Cline's killer avante garde guitar, Glen Koche's innovative percussion and John Stiratt's melodic yet restrained bass lines) are no longer so apparent, rather they are still there but more subtle. I know it sounds weird but Nels Cline's critical guitar is there, but it's not there! Everything that is great about Wilco is still there but now, you must be a more astute listener to find it. B0059XTTSM|5.0|but I'll try not to play the spoiler. Suffice it to say, the full meaning of Take Shelter rests perilously upon the final scene, where the primary issue of the film--the sanity of the protagonist--is seemingly resolved. A straight-forward reading of that scene yields a richness of social implication; allegorical or ironic readings yield nothing but the fruit of a well-acted psycho-suspense thriller.<br /><br />Appearances aside, this is not a film about the private affairs of a married couple, and this is not a film that ends on a note of discontinuity. The whole point of the film is that the line between private anguish and public life is an illusion; the whole point of the ending is that it is the logical outcome of a psychological process that has been building up for two hours on screen.<br /><br />As far as I'm concerned, Take Shelter is about the challenge of people coming together in the face of an impending political catastrophe, about forging a meeting-of-minds that requires nothing less than extraordinary courage and empathy from all of us, as we strive to overcome the all-but-unbridgeable gaps between our various subjective experiences of the world. In this sense, the super-natural elements in Take Shelter appear only superficially in the sky; the real miracle is in what happens between two human beings--married or unmarried, it makes no difference--by the end of the film.<br /><br /> In my view, Take Shelter is far and away the most important meta-political film of this young century.

The first problem I see is privacy issues that go along with yet another system for managing media. I have a large distrust for companies ability to keep the data safe, not that I think that they have much interest in my entertainment preferences, beyond selling me more. I have rather serious reservations about being forced to sign an agreement that allows them to make changes to it without my knowledge(which most of these license agreements state). As the current market leader, Apple's business is big enough, and they are smart enough to realize that they would loose millions if they were breached. That fact makes me believe they will stop at nothing to protect that information. A startup competitor might cut corners in order to get a new system up and running.  The next issue is how long it took to wade through the setup process. In my case, with a new laptop, wideband internet and a fairly well developed technical ability, it was over an hour from start to finish. Granted, much of that time would not be an issue if I were predisposed to utilizing the system again. What follows is the process...  Setup Flixster account with password  Agree to Flixster's terms of service, privacy rules  Setup Ultraviolet account with password  Agree to Ultraviolet terms of service and privacy rules  Download and install Flixster Collections application  Download and install Adobe Air  Agree to AdobeAir Licensing agreement  Agree to Flixter's End User Agreement(which couldn't be completely viewed or printed!)  Enter in the code in the BluRay box.  Download the movie  The deal breaker for me was the lack of iPod transfer. I like to have the movies available for the kids to watch in the car, and there is no way that I could find to transfer this type of digital copy to an iPod. Any sort of digital copy has to have this option for me, or else it looses significant value.  All in all, I understand that the movie studios want to keep the profits of distribution, instead of giving them to Apple, but in order to get me to switch, they are going to need to make the process easier and provide iPod access as well. I will be avoiding all digital downloads that use the UltraViolet system. Maybe they can fix the issues, but I'm dubious that will happen.
